DEBUG LOG STARTED


--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).

When planning acquisitions or such other corporate transactions, the acquiring party will usually have to consider how it prefers to structure the transactions. The acquiring party can either elect to purchase the target company's assets (which enables the acquiring party to pick and choose only the assets and contracts they desire) or alternatively it may elect to acquire the entire company. In an asset sale, the need for contract-by-contract due diligence, would become particularly important since each contract needs to be specifically assigned in the context of asset sale. The acquiring party would generally be able to avoid such issues by electing to acquire the entire share capital of the target company (thereby making it a subsidiary of the acquiring entity) or by merging together with the target company. In the latter case (i.e. when the acquiring party obtains all outstanding shares in the target company and makes the acquired company its subsidiary), there is strictly speaking no need to obtain a consent for the assignment of the related contracts. This is because the contracts in themselves are not \u2018assigned\u2019 to any new legal entity but only the owner of the existing legal entity changes. Contrary to widely held belief, changes in ownership do not generally require the other contracting party\u2019s consent. In companies whose shares are stock-listed the ownership changes each day the market is open.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 36, "text": "An acquisition or a merger would in most countries serve to \"vest\" all assets and liabilities including contracts automatically to a merging and surviving entity or the acquiring party, as the case may be, by \u2018operation of law\u2019 without a need to obtain a consent from the opposing party. For example, if Zebracorp was planning on forming a merger with another company, we would work from the assumption that our contracts with our suppliers/subcontractors would be automatically transferred to the new merged entity by operation of law without the need seek consents from our supplier base. Under UK laws, an assignment of rights under a contract is normally restricted solely to the benefits of the contract. Where a party wishes to transfer both the benefit and burden of the contract without any residual liability (as is usually the case), the assignment of the contract would generally needs to be done by way of a novation. For this reason, when the agreement is governed by UK laws, it is useful to specifically mention that the agreement is not only assigned but also novated, and the transfer of the rights and obligations may require the execution of a specific novation agreement. Please speak to a member of the Legal & Compliance team to obtain the appropriate templates/language. Due to the prevalence of UK origin contract documentation, the term novation and novation agreements in general are often (incorrectly) used in other jurisdictions as well when the parties should be talking about contract transfer or assignment. These type of changes in the name or registered addresses of a company are not regarded as assignments so long as the underlying legal entity remains the same and only the name/address of the company changes. Accordingly, there is no need to seek or grant consent to such change of name/registered address alone.  A unilateral notification of the name change to the other contracting party should be sufficient. While it is recommended to clarify the name change in future contract amendments, strictly speaking this is not necessary. Widely used variants to the phrase include \u2018commercially reasonable efforts\u2019, \u2018commercially reasonable best efforts\u2019, \u2018good-faith efforts, \u2018all reasonable efforts\u2019 where the term \u2018efforts\u2019 is often replaced with the term \u2018endeavours\u2019. In each case the intent of the phrase is to remove the absolute duty or commitment to accomplish certain specific obligation or target. Understandably these phrases are often introduced by vendors in an attempt to soften their liability to meet some of the obligations that a purchasing agreement imposes on such vendors. As an example a product vendor may wish rephrase an obligation to meet Zebracorp quality requirements and standards to an obligation to make merely commercially reasonable efforts to meet such quality requirements and standards.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 48, "text": "The legal meaning of these phrases varies from country to country. As an example under NY USA laws the phrase \u2018best efforts\u2019 basically only puts a good faith obligation on party do try to meet the prescribed obligation. Failure to accomplish such best efforts obligation would generally not be a breach of contract. This is because these phrases are considered too vague to create a binding enforceable obligation. This is in stark contrast to some of the common law countries where these phrases have more binding nature. The view generally held by many US lawyers is that party promising to do \u2018best efforts\u2019 is required to do everything in its power to accomplish the target even if it bankrupts itself in the process. You should therefore expect in particular US based vendors to replace \u2018best efforts\u2019 with less onerous efforts standards such as in e.g. \u2018commercial reasonable efforts\u2019. As a rule of thumb we should be able to accept these less stringent variants whenever we would have been willing to accept the phrase \u2018best efforts\u2019.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 49, "text": "In all cases it is important to keep in mind that the phrase \u2018best efforts\u2019 together with all its variants are ultimately very vague and do not provide a firm commitment to meet the agreed requirement or obligation. Hence, we should, despite their prevalence, be careful in accepting such phrases. As an example, when a supplier is hesitant as to whether it can comply with in e.g. all of the Zebracorp quality requirements or Zebracorp environmental requirements, it would be recommend to ask the supplier to identify which specific requirements the supplier is unable to meet and revise those as needed, instead of simply saying that the supplier will use best efforts or commercially reasonable effort to meet such requirements. The odds are that the supplier has no issues in committing itself to the majority of the requirements but only has reservations on some of the requirements. By requiring the supplier to identify those requirements that the supplier is unable to comply with, we often receive valuable information on the technical and other capabilities of the supplier products/solutions - information which would be lost if we merely accept the \u2018best effort\u2019 qualifier.", "style": "Normal"}]

--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).

[{"index": 45, "text": "\u201cExcept as otherwise provided in this [Agreement], neither party may assign, novate, delegate, or transfer this [Agreement], or any of its individual rights or obligations under this [Agreement], without the prior written approval of the other Party, which approval will not be unreasonably withheld or delayed. However, [Zebracorp] may, without the prior written consent of the [Supplier], unconditionally assign, novate, delegate or transfer any or all of its rights and obligations under this [Agreement], in whole, in part, or through a splitting of the [Agreement], to any Affiliate or to any acquirer of a Zebracorp business line to which this contract relates. As of the effective date of the assignment, novation, delegation, or transfer, the assignee entity(ies) assumes all of the relevant rights, obligations and liabilities of the [Agreement], and [Zebracorp] is unconditionally released and discharged from all of the relevant rights, obligations and liabilities of the [Agreement].  [Supplier] will work cooperatively with [Zebracorp] and the assignee entity(ies) to ensure a smooth and orderly transition.\u201d", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 46, "text": "\u2018BEST EFFORTS\u2019 PROVISIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 47, "text": "Contract provisions that use the phrase \u2018best efforts\u2019 or one of its variants are often a source of confusion when negotiating agreements. Widely used variants to the phrase include \u2018commercially reasonable efforts\u2019, \u2018commercially reasonable best efforts\u2019, \u2018good-faith efforts, \u2018all reasonable efforts\u2019 where the term \u2018efforts\u2019 is often replaced with the term \u2018endeavours\u2019. In each case the intent of the phrase is to remove the absolute duty or commitment to accomplish certain specific obligation or target. Understandably these phrases are often introduced by vendors in an attempt to soften their liability to meet some of the obligations that a purchasing agreement imposes on such vendors. As an example a product vendor may wish rephrase an obligation to meet Zebracorp quality requirements and standards to an obligation to make merely commercially reasonable efforts to meet such quality requirements and standards.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 48, "text": "The legal meaning of these phrases varies from country to country. As an example under NY USA laws the phrase \u2018best efforts\u2019 basically only puts a good faith obligation on party do try to meet the prescribed obligation. Failure to accomplish such best efforts obligation would generally not be a breach of contract. This is because these phrases are considered too vague to create a binding enforceable obligation. This is in stark contrast to some of the common law countries where these phrases have more binding nature. The view generally held by many US lawyers is that party promising to do \u2018best efforts\u2019 is required to do everything in its power to accomplish the target even if it bankrupts itself in the process. You should therefore expect in particular US based vendors to replace \u2018best efforts\u2019 with less onerous efforts standards such as in e.g. \u2018commercial reasonable efforts\u2019. As a rule of thumb we should be able to accept these less stringent variants whenever we would have been willing to accept the phrase \u2018best efforts\u2019.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 49, "text": "In all cases it is important to keep in mind that the phrase \u2018best efforts\u2019 together with all its variants are ultimately very vague and do not provide a firm commitment to meet the agreed requirement or obligation. Hence, we should, despite their prevalence, be careful in accepting such phrases. As an example, when a supplier is hesitant as to whether it can comply with in e.g. all of the Zebracorp quality requirements or Zebracorp environmental requirements, it would be recommend to ask the supplier to identify which specific requirements the supplier is unable to meet and revise those as needed, instead of simply saying that the supplier will use best efforts or commercially reasonable effort to meet such requirements. The odds are that the supplier has no issues in committing itself to the majority of the requirements but only has reservations on some of the requirements. --- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).


CURRENT DOCUMENT TYPE: MASTER

--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).

Arbitration has benefits over litigation in local courts also due to the fact that arbitration awards (that is the final \u2018judgements\u2019 given by arbitration panel) issued in one country are usually much less complex and timely to enforce in another country. The enforceability of court judgement/arbitration award is major consideration in determining the appropriate dispute resolution form and venue. As an example, if Zebracorp and the supplier have agreed to settle their disputes in local courts as opposed to arbitration and, subsequent to a dispute, Zebracorp prevails in such dispute and obtains a court decision where the supplier is ordered to pay damages to Zebracorp, the enforcement of the local court decision may meet unexpected obstacles. If the supplier is not resident in the country where Zebracorp obtained such favourable court decision, it may prove next to impossible for Zebracorp to enforce the court decisions against the supplier. The local courts in the country of residence of the supplier may not be willing to recognise the validity of a court decision issued in a foreign country, which effectively renders Zebracorp\u2019s earlier court victory practically worthless. The enforceability of foreign court judgements is usually dependent on bilateral treaties between the concerned countries, and is at best uncertain.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 106, "text": "The enforcement of foreign arbitration awards, on the other hand, has been made much less cumbersome by the wide adoption of a United Nations Convention on the Recognition and Enforcement of Foreign Arbitral Awards, also known as the New York Convention. This is because arbitration awards rendered in a non-member country might not be enforceable in other countries.  The place of arbitration should preferably be same as the governing law of the agreement. As an example, if the agreement is governed by the laws of England, we would, for all practical purposes, prefer that the venue of arbitration is in e.g. in London or other major city in the UK. Having the venue of arbitration in a distant or unfamiliar location, may make it difficult to find suitable qualified arbitrators that are familiar with the governing law of the agreement.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 109, "text": "It is always recommended to agree the number of arbitrators in the arbitration clause, albeit some Arbitration bodies (such as those of the ICC International Court of Arbitration) provide that the arbitration body can decide the number of arbitrators based on the particular circumstances of the dispute if the arbitration clause is silent on the matter. When determining the number of arbitrators, it is fairly common to agree on either one or three arbitrators. The key is to ensure that we have an odd number of arbitrators, since an arbitration panel having an even number of arbitrators may not be able to agree and in consequence there will be no majority opinion. While there are some cost advantages of opting for a single arbitrator, it is often recommended to appoint three arbitrators. These clauses are typically found in the \u2018Final Provisions\u2019 or \u2018Miscellaneous\u2019 sections towards the end of the agreements. Though rarely subject to negotiations, these \u2018Entire Agreement\u2019 clauses are an important part of each agreement. The purpose of the \u2018Entire Agreement\u2019 clause is to strengthen the written purchase agreement, and to make sure that any emails or other correspondence made prior to the signing of the final agreement are superseded by the terms of the written agreement. An \u2018Entire Agreement\u2019 clause will prevent a seller from later in e.g. defending its mistakes by saying essentially in e.g. that \u201cZEBRACORP\u2019s quality representatives told us to do it differently\u201d. More importantly, these clauses also prevent the supplier/subcontractor from in e.g. claiming that any estimates or implied promises that ZEBRACORP has made on future business or purchasing volumes before the signing of the agreement, should be binding on ZEBRACORP. Much of the Zebracorp product environmental requirements are derived from the European Union Environmental Directives. This means that the component suppliers can legally sell Zebracorp non-RoHS compliant components and subassemblies, but we as Zebracorp cannot sell products in EU market with such non-RoHS compliant components. The result of this is that Zebracorp bears the responsibility for ensuring that any and all materials and substances used in Zebracorp products are fully compliant with all applicable environmental laws and regulations. Also, if Zebracorp is the first vendor, who puts a product on the market in the EU, Zebracorp is also responsible for ensuring such Product\u2019s compliance with EU RoHS Directive (i.e. importer to EU is considered to be a producer/manufacturer in EU). Recycling and disposal instructions are also mandatory. The recycling rules are however different to b-to-b products and b-to-c products. Zebracorp\u2019s subcontractors should assume liability for these costs relating to the product they have developed and manufactured, in particular taking into account they could have developed and designed the product in the manner the disposal and recycling is easy and efficient. Note the collection is the most expensive part and the disposal is the less expensive part (sometimes even positive).", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 123, "text": "EPIDEMIC FAILURE", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 124, "text": "General. Epidemic Failure clauses define how the seller will repay and reimburse the costs incurred for certain systematic or excessive defects. Epidemic Failure clauses differ from product warranty provisions in that they generally (i) last substantially longer than warranty and (ii) provide remedies in addition to \u201crepair, replacement or credit.\u201d", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 125, "text": "Another important practical difference between the warranty clause and the Epidemic Failure clause is that, while the warranty applies to and provides a remedy for products known to be defective, the Epidemic Failure clause also applies to products suspected of being defective.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 126, "text": "An epidemic/systematic defect typically has the following characteristics:", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 127, "text": "The defect repeats itself in (many/several) products (as opposite to single failures);", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 128, "text": "The defect has the same or similar root cause;", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 129, "text": "The defect is often (but not always) subject to certain pre-defined threshold (\u201cx % of products fail\u201d);", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 130, "text": "The defect may require retrofit action where ALL affected products are replaced (including those where the defect has not appeared) and vendor\u2019s cost and expense.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 131, "text": "The main argument for an Epidemic Failure clause is that warranty provisions alone are insufficient to compensate the purchasing party for (i) any costs of a product retrofit (=field replacement) or recall should one be required due to the systematic nature of the defect ; (ii) similar clauses/obligations are commonly used in the industrial automation business between us and the customers, which obligations we simply cannot avoid; (iii) the products are (typically) already in the field and not in manufacturing line or warehouses, and therefore the replacement is not enough to cover the occurred costs; and (vi) Zebracorp industrial products have a long life time and the epidemic/systematic defects typically do not appear at the early times in the field, and (v) the epidemic/systematic failure costs occur due to subcontractor\u2019s product (design, manufacturing or component) and not due to Zebracorp\u2019s fault. Typically epidemic/systematic failures cannot be identified in R&D testing.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 132, "text": "Software errors or defects arising from ordinary wear and tear are not generally viewed as Epidemic Failures and these can be often expressly excluded from the scope of an Epidemic Failure provision.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 133, "text": "More information on dealing with retrofit cases can be found under the below link:   https://www.zebracorp.com/retrofit.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 134, "text": "Definition. Triggers. It is difficult to define the appropriate triggers for an Epidemic Failure. Usually the triggers include:", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 135, "text": "A certain percentage of defects found during a particular time frame. As an example, we can agree that Epidemic Failure shall be deemed to take place in the event of a failure occurring in [XX] percent or more of total number of products purchased within [YY] number of months or days. The numbers can be adjusted depending on the nature of the products. Obviously the higher the percentage threshold, the less likely the Epidemic Failure clause can be used. It is often recommended to have more than one trigger. As an example we could agree, that a failure rate equal or above [XX] % during any given one-month period or a failure rate equal or above [XX] % during any given six-month period, will trigger an Epidemic Failure.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 136, "text": "Sophisticated vendors will prefer to insert a requirement that certain minimum number of units must found to be defective in order to trigger Epidemic Failure. This is mostly acceptable. For example, if the trigger is 1%, but only 200 units were sold, then two defective units whose defect is attributable to the same root cause would constitute an Epidemic Failure because 1% of 200 is two. These anomalies can be eliminated by adding a provision that requires there to be a minimum number (e.g., a minimum of 10 defective units) in order to trigger the Epidemic Failure clause.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 137, "text": "Note that the above triggers are typical examples only. There is no single correct way to define an Epidemic Failure but the right definition varies from case-to-case. The right triggers is ultimately dependent on the nature of the products. If you need to change the proposed failure rate percentages in the templates, please check it with the relevant Product unit that is familiar with the product in question and understands the customer systems (Customer Support (technical support)). The key for Zebracorp is that the definition is not overly restrictive, and does not thereby prevent Zebracorp from calling on retrofit where retrofit is clearly required to resolve the issue. In some instances an acceptable outcome can be found by using a dictionary definition of epidemic failure, and to define it as any design or manufacturing defect in the products that is systematic in nature and affects multiple products. Should this dictionary definition be seen as too broad by the supplier, we can narrow it down by stating that such defects need to adversely affect Zebracorp customer deliveries, or to have a material adverse effect on Zebracorp or its customers. The downside with this type of dictionary definitions, or any generic definitions, is that they also permit the supplier to challenge their applicability. However, they may be viable option in the event the parties are unable to find a more structured definition with clear numerical values/triggers. Those triggers may also prove problematic as they may arbitrarily rule out the possibility of retrofit if these triggers are overly generous towards the suppliers.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 138, "text": "Remedies. Liabilities. In addition to the definition of the Epidemic Failure, another key consideration, when negotiating an Epidemic Failure clause, is the remedies available to Zebracorp when such failures do occur. Most vendors will seek to limit their liability for assistance and support in resolving the issue without assuming any firm financial liability other than by mutual agreement. Such limitations are rarely reasonable or acceptable. The fact is that Epidemic Failure clauses are commonplace in the industry and our end customers expect Zebracorp to be committed to implement certain corrective measures in the event of such a failure of epidemic nature at Zebracorp\u2019s cost. Zebracorp should at a minimum require the supplier/vendor of a product to be liable for A. the repair/replacement of all of the affected products; B. the de-installation and reinstallation work in the location/site where the products were used; and C. all associated transportation and logistics costs and expenses. Note that the liability of the supplier should specifically include all \u2018affected\u2019 products and not only all defective products. The purpose of the field repair/replacement is often to also to repair/replace those products that have been found to have the same root defect and that are therefore likely to fail even if these products may be still perfectly functioning.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 139, "text": "Epidemic Failure clause can be hard to negotiate because of the substantial financial liability and risk involved for the seller. It is often advisable to query whether the seller has product recall/retrofit insurance available and, if so, whether it provides appropriate coverage.  Another way of making the Epidemic Failure clause more palatable for the supplier, is to include a specific monetary cap on the supplier\u2019s liability under the Epidemic Failure provision. Note however that any such specific liability cap should be aligned and take into account when agreeing on an overall liability cap for the entire contract. Please see more on liability caps in the Section titled \u2018Limitation of Liability\u2019 below.", "style": "Normal"}]

--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).

--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).


CURRENT DOCUMENT TYPE: REFERENCE

A \u2018Force Majeure\u2019 clause is a contract provision that allows a party to suspend or terminate the performance of its obligations when certain circumstances beyond their control arise, making performance impossible, commercially impracticable or, in some extreme cases, illegal.  The provision may state that the contract is temporarily suspended, or that it is terminated if the event of force majeure continues for a prescribed period of time.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 152, "text": "Definition. Events of Force Majeure. Force Majeure is defined generally as any event or condition, A. not existing as of the date of signature of the contract, B. not reasonably foreseeable as of such date and not reasonably within the control of either party, C. external to the contract and the parties  i.e. not caused by a party and D. which prevents, in whole or in significant part, the performance by one of the parties of its contractual obligations, or which renders the performance of such obligations so difficult or costly as to make such performance commercially unreasonable. Gross negligence is often also termed as wilful negligence. It involves a higher level of misconduct than ordinary negligence and requires proof of something more than the lack of ordinary care. An example of grossly negligent conduct would be a company permitting a product to be sold for a particular end-use despite knowing that the product is unsafe or unsuitable for such specific purpose.", "style": "Normal"}, {"index": 176, "text": "Commercial contracts usually prevent a party from insulating itself from paying damages caused by its grossly negligent conduct. --- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).

--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).

--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).

--- CHUNK START ---
You are a legal document structure analyzer.
Your task is to classify text blocks from a contract into specific categories based on the document type.

**Input Format:**
You will receive a JSON list of blocks. Each block has an `index`, `text`, and optionally `style`.
Example: `[{"index": 0, "text": "1. DEFINITIONS", "style": "Heading 1"}, {"index": 1, "text": "In this agreement..."}]`

**Context Awareness:**
You may receive a `PREVIOUS CONTEXT` field. This tells you the state of the document immediately before the current chunk.
- If the previous chunk ended inside a section (e.g., "Table of Contents"), you MUST continue that section unless a clear new header appears.
- Do NOT start a new `CLAUSE_START` if the text is clearly a continuation of a list or table from the previous section.

**Style Rules:**
- If `style` contains "TOC", "Table of Contents", or "Index", classify it as `CONTENT` (or `INFO_START` if it's the main header).
- Do NOT classify TOC items as `CLAUSE_START`.

**Strict Rules:**
- **Annex vs Appendix**: Do NOT confuse them.
    - If the text says "Annex", use `ANNEX_START`.
    - If the text says "Appendix", use `APPENDIX_START`.
    - If the text says "Schedule", use `APPENDIX_START` (unless specifically instructed otherwise, but usually Schedule = Appendix).
- **Plural Headers**: Generic plural headers like "APPENDICES", "SCHEDULES", or "EXHIBITS" (without a specific letter/number) are `INFO_START`. Do NOT tag them as `APPENDIX_START`.
- **Table Continuity**:
    - If you are inside a section (e.g., Annex) and see text that looks like a table row (e.g., "| Col 1 | Col 2 |"), tag it as `CONTENT`.
    - **Ignore Repeated Headers**: If you see headers like "Table continued", "Page X", or repeated column names inside a table, tag them as `CONTENT`. Do NOT start a new section.

**Output Format:**
Return a JSON list of objects. Each object must have:
- `index`: The EXACT integer index from the input block.
- `type`: The classification tag.

Example: `[{"index": 0, "type": "INFO"}, {"index": 1, "type": "CLAUSE"}]`

**Allowed Tags & Rules:**

If Document Type is **MASTER** or **SUBORDINATE**:
**Goal:** Group the document into high-level sections. Do NOT split sub-sections.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-contractual section.
    -   Examples: Preamble, Recitals, Table of Contents, Party Details, Signature Pages.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the first block (header/title) of this section.

2.  `CLAUSE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a main contractual clause.
    -   Examples: "1. DEFINITIONS", "2. TERM", "ARTICLE 3: FEES".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the main header (e.g. "1.", "Article I"). Do NOT tag sub-headers (e.g. "1.1") with this.

3.  `APPENDIX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Appendix.
    -   Examples: "APPENDIX A", "APPENDIX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

4.  `ANNEX_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Annex.
    -   Examples: "ANNEX A", "ANNEX 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

5.  `EXHIBIT_START`: Use this for the **START** of an Exhibit.
    -   Examples: "EXHIBIT A", "EXHIBIT 1".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

6.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, sub-clauses (1.1, 1.1.1), list items, and content following a header.
    -   This content will be grouped under the preceding `_START` tag.

If Document Type is **REFERENCE**:
**Goal:** Identify Guidelines.

**Allowed Tags:**

1.  `INFO_START`: Use this for the **START** of a non-guideline section.
    -   Examples: Title, Introduction, Scope.
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

2.  `GUIDELINE_START`: Use this for the **START** of a Guideline section.
    -   Examples: "Guideline 1", "Policy A", "Standard 1.2".
    -   Rule: Tag ONLY the header.

3.  `CONTENT`: Use this for **EVERYTHING ELSE**.
    -   Body text, list items, and content following a header.

**Prohibited Tags for REFERENCE:**
-   Do NOT use `CLAUSE_START`, `APPENDIX_START`, `ANNEX_START`, or `EXHIBIT_START`.

**Critical Instructions:**
1.  **Preserve Indices**: Return a classification for EVERY block.
2.  **No IDs**: Do NOT generate `id` fields.
3.  **One Section = One Start Tag**: Do not fragment a section. For example, "1. Definitions" gets `CLAUSE_START`. "1.1" gets `CONTENT`. "1.2" gets `CONTENT`. This keeps them all in one "Clause 1" block.
4.  **Appendices**: "APPENDIX A" gets `APPENDIX_START`. All text inside it gets `CONTENT`.
5.  **Ignore References**: Do NOT use `_START` tags for mentions in a Table of Contents. Use `CONTENT` or `INFO_START` (if it's the TOC header).


CURRENT DOCUMENT TYPE: REFERENCE

An indemnity is essentially a contractual obligation resting on one party (Indemnitor) to make good a loss or damage incurred by another party (Indemnitee).  The indemnity provision allows the parties to agree on a set of costs and damages that must be reimbursed in the event certain facts or circumstances occur. In a standard action for breach of contract, the amount of money that may be recovered by the claimant is limited by various legal filters, such as a requirement that the damages must be foreseeable, unavoidable and reasonable. In the event the contract specifies that certain damages will be recoverable on an indemnity basis, then the level of damages will depend upon the drafting of the indemnity, and the damages need not necessarily be limited by the above referred filters. Hardware may easily infringe patents, or registered designs and even copyrights depending on the country/region. Also software frequently embodies methods of operation and may infringe both copyright and patents. With the large number of patents being applied for and granted, patent infringements claims in particular have become mundane within the IT industry. In contrast to copyright infringement where infringement customarily requires intentional or knowing copying of someone else\u2019s copyrighted material, patent infringement can occur without any culpable behaviour on the part of the infringer. Knowledge of the existing patent or an intention to infringe the patent is by and large irrelevant.
